Danish Title:
Anvendelse af Generativ Kunstig Intelligens i Designprocesser
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This master’s study investigates how generative artiﬁcial intelligence (AI) can be integrated
into the design process, focusing on the double diamond framework. It aims to develop a
user-friendly prototype that e↵ectively empowers designers to identify and leverage gener-
ative AI opportunities. The ﬁnal problem statement explored is: How might we create a
minimum viable product of a solution that helps designers identify generative AI opportuni-
ties within the double diamond framework? By answering this question, the study creates a
proposal for designers to be equipped with the knowledge and strategies to utilize AI e↵ec-
tively, ultimately improving design outcomes and fostering innovation.
The study methodology combines qualitative and quantitative approaches, including 14 in-
terviews, 23 questionnaires, and an experimental pilot study using ChatGPT on writing
tasks. The ﬁndings reveal designers’ generally optimistic outlook on generative AI’s poten-
tial to enhance design eﬃciency and creativity. However, concerns regarding ethical use,
copyright issues, and over-reliance on AI tools were also identiﬁed.
The study’s outcome is a user-centered online platform serving as a minimum viable proto-
type. This platform provides a structured overview of where potential beneﬁts are identiﬁed
for integrating generative AI within the double diamond framework. It o↵ers general sug-
gestions and concrete best practice examples and communicates responsible generative AI
use. This solution emerged from developing and analyzing multiple concept prototypes and
incorporates feedback from early-stage user testing with design and innovation students.
This study contributes to understanding how generative AI can be strategically integrated
into design processes to boost innovation and eﬃciency. The minimum viable prototype de-
veloped o↵ers a practical tool for designers to navigate the evolving landscape of generative
AI, ensuring they can leverage its potential responsibly and creatively.
Future work will focus on evolving the prototype into a fully functional product through
further testing, validation, development and integration, potentially into design education
with a collaboration with design institutions to enhance students’ AI capabilities. Adapting
to the rapidly changing generative AI landscape is a critical challenge that requires on-
going attention to ensure that solutions remain relevant and e↵ective in enhancing design
practices.
Preface
The master’s study, titled Application of Generative Artiﬁcial Intelligence in Design Pro-
cesses is written by Lydia Elkær Lynggaard, s183657, with supervision from Per Bækgaard
and Dagn´y Valgeirsd´ottir, with Dagn´y substituted by John Paulin Hansen from start Febru-
ary 2024. The project is 30 ECTS points and is carried out from the 1st of September to
14th of March 2024, including a six-week pause due to sickness. It serves as the ﬁnal project
in a Design and Innovation education and is a part of DTU Institute for Mathematics and
Computer Science.
The project explores how designers can beneﬁt from generative AI tools and how generative
AI integration can be presented to designers. The aim of the study is to provide designers
with speciﬁc suggestions on how to utilize generative AI tools. The motivation for this study
stems from a blend of professional curiosity, the current relevance of the topic, and a personal
p. 1 of 80
==PAGE:4==
interest in learning how to gain advantages of AI tools. This is particularly relevant as a
soon to be graduate in design engineering.
I want to delegate a thanks to the people who have made this master’s study possible for me,
including the interview and questionnaire participants, who have shared their experiences
and time, and the test persons who have provided valuable feedback on the early concepts.
I would like to express my gratitude to my competent supervisors, Per Bækgaard, Dagn´y
Valgeirsd´ottir, and John Paulin Hansen, who have provided not only academic advisement
but also emotional support throughout that made this thesis possible for me. Furthermore,
I would like to thank my supporting family and friends highlighting Sophie Friis Henriksen
and Emma Lange. Lastly, I would like to thank my supporting loved one, Frederik Buk
Henriksen.
I hope this project will be part of the journey to a better understanding of generative AI
tools and how to utilize them among designers.
Reading Guide
This Master’s study is structured according to the IMRaD format for easy navigation [35].
The study’s sections are divided into the IMRaD framework’s phases in Figure 1.
Figure 1: The study’s sections are divided in the IMRaD report structure.
It is written concurrently with the project’s development, which is iterative rather than
linear. This means that as the project has evolved, both models, methods, and text are
continuously revisited and reﬁned. The paper is divided into several sections, each of which
has its own objective and contributes to the overall progress of the project. Each section be-
gins by outlining its objective and the methodology used to achieve it. Finally, each section
concludes with a description of the results achieved towards the objective of that section.
Throughout the paper, the text is supported by ﬁgures and tables that communicate in line
with the text. Much of the analysis and conclusions are illustrated in the ﬁgures. All ﬁgures
and tables have been created by the author, either manually or using AI tools.
The text is written in columns to improve readability and enhance aesthetics. In certain
paragraphs, sentences highlighted in blue emphasize key points within the text to facilitate
easy navigation. The document uses cross-references throughout to link related sections,
ﬁgures, and tables for easier navigation and a deeper understanding. For further elaboration
and detailed material, readers are directed to the appendix, referred to as ’Appx. XX.’ Here,
’XX’ denotes the speciﬁc appendix number. An overview of the appendix is provided at the
end of the table of contents.

1
INTRODUCTION
1
Introduction
Innovation has always been at the core of
human progress, driving us towards im-
provement and optimization. This drive for
innovative progression is especially evident
in the technology progression.
In recent
years, the development of Artiﬁcial Intelli-
gence (AI) technology has gone through a
major breakthrough.
AI has transitioned
from being a subject of scientiﬁc research to
the global limelight, becoming an integral
part of our daily lives and conversations.
Today, AI has become an unavoidable actor
present in all aspects of life, from algorithms
curating social media feeds to autonomous
vacuum cleaners and within almost all ar-
eas of interest. Its unavoidable presence is
necessitating our engagement and adapta-
tion. Microsoft founder Bill Gates resonates
with AI’s transformative impact on daily
routines:
”It (AI) will change the way
people work, learn, travel, get
health care, and communicate
with each other. Entire industries
will reorient around it. Businesses
will distinguish themselves by how
well they use it.” – Bill Gates,
Microsoft founder, March 21, 2023
[14]
The quote by Bill Gates emphasizes how AI
inﬂuences the functional aspects of work as
well as personal life and how businesses and
industries deﬁne and position themselves.
It would be unreasonable to believe that
the design process is an exception to this
AI revolution, where particularly genera-
tive AI (gen AI) is rapidly evolving.
This anticipation of change emphasizes de-
signers’ need to incorporate AI into their
design processes to keep up with the rapidly
evolving technology inﬂuencing industries.
Inspired by this evolution, the study ad-
dresses the question:
How might we help
designers integrate and beneﬁt from gen AI
in the design process?
This problem statement aims to explore the
integration of gen AI in the design indus-
try to help designers unlock the potential of
gen AI to enhance creativity, eﬃciency, and
innovation within their design process while
communicating AI concerns and limitations.
To achieve this goal, this study employs the
double diamond design framework, guid-
ing the master’s process through diverging
and converging phases. The study combines
qualitative and quantitative research meth-
ods, including desk studies, surveys, and in-
terviews, to capture designers’ perceptions
of AI. A pilot study on text generators
evaluates gen AI’s impact, which, together
with ﬁeldwork insights, leads to developing
a minimum viable prototype (MVP) com-
municating gen AI integration opportunities
to designers.
In response, this thesis proposes an on-
line platform prototype designed to enable
designers to identify opportunities for gen
AI integration within the double diamond
framework.
This platform o↵ers a struc-
tured visual overview of the double dia-
mond framework, with focused communica-
tion on how gen AI can enhance various de-
sign methods, including integrated, tailored
gen AI versions.
Moreover, this platform
communicates use cases for responsible and
e↵ective use of gen AI. By providing gen-
eral suggestions and concrete best practice
examples, the platform aims to serve as a
dynamic resource for designers to beneﬁt
from gen AI.
Despite the study’s non-linear process, this
master’s paper is structured to guide the
reader linearly through the ﬁndings, elabo-
rating how each discovery informs the next
and providing a clear narrative of the re-
search journey and its contributions to the
solution.
p. 5 of 80
==PAGE:8==
2
BACKGROUND
2
Background
This background section provides a thor-
ough overview of the design process and gen-
erative AI (gen AI) that this Master’s study
is targeting. This involves exploring the cur-
rent state of research, exploring and reﬂect-
ing on the design process, and understand-
ing the fundamental principles of gen AI to
identify its possibilities and limitations. Ad-
ditionally, an analysis of available gen AI
tools is conducted.
2.1
Design Processes
This subsection deﬁnes the interpretation of
the term ’design process’ within this Mas-
ter’s thesis.
It involves an in-depth ex-
ploration and reﬂective analysis of various
design thinking frameworks to understand
their application and value in guiding a de-
sign process.
Furthermore, it involves an
exploration of typical steps in a design pro-
cess serving as the as-is journey.
A design process is a zoomed-out frame-
work to create products, services, product
services, or strategies.
Numerous design
processes exist, and each design process of-
ten has multiple variations.
Within design and innovation engineering,
design thinking is a trusted and familiar
human-centered mindset and process to
solve problems and needs by creating inno-
vative solutions. But what is design think-
ing?
And is it really that valuable?
The
term ’design thinking’ is widely discussed
and interpreted in di↵erent ways.
Even
the famous cognitive scientist and designer
Donald Arthur Norman has grappled with
and reconsidered his perspective on design
thinking. In his article ’Design Thinking: A
Useful Myth’, [36] Norman takes a critical
perspective on design thinking, viewing it
as a misleading term sustained by the de-
sign community and consultancies to serve
marketing purposes rather than reﬂecting
reality. However, this viewpoint changes in
his subsequent article, ’Rethinking Design
Thinking’, [37] where he supports design
thinking as a systematic and valuable ap-
proach to problem-solving:
”Designers have developed a
number of techniques to avoid
being captured by too facile a
solution. They take the original
problem as a suggestion, not as a
ﬁnal statement, then think broadly
about what the real issues
underlying this problem statement
might really be ... Designers resist
the temptation to jump
immediately to a solution to the
stated problem. Instead, they ﬁrst
spend time determining what the
basic, fundamental (root) issue is
that needs to be addressed. They
don’t try to search for a solution
until they have determined the real
problem, and even then, instead of
solving that problem, they stop to
consider a wide range of potential
solutions. Only then will they
ﬁnally converge upon their
proposal. This process is called
”Design Thinking.” - Don
Norman, cognitive scientist and
designer, March 19, 2013 [37]
Norman’s statement showcases his changed
perspective on design thinking as a strong
approach to understanding the underlying
root cause issues of a problem before explor-
ing a wide range of potential solutions.
2.1.1
Design Thinking Frameworks
This subsection aims to ensure the study’s
solution is compatible with various design
processes and, thus, various design thinking
frameworks. To achieve this, it investigates
and ﬁnds patterns within di↵erent design
thinking frameworks.
To investigate design thinking frameworks,
ChatGPT 4.0 was tasked to list popular de-
sign thinking frameworks. As output, Chat-
GPT 4.0 provided four frameworks, namely,
the ﬁve stages, three core activities, the loop,
p. 6 of 80
==PAGE:9==
2.1
Design Processes
2
BACKGROUND
and Google Ventures (GV) design sprint.
These will be explained and compared to
the double diamond framework, which is
the commonly used framework within de-
sign and innovation engineering.
A widely known design thinking framework
is the ﬁve stage framework, developed by
Stanford University’s Institute of Design
[39].
As the name implies, this frame-
work deﬁnes the design process through ﬁve
stages: emphasize, deﬁne, ideate, prototype,
and test, as visualized and explained in Fig-
ure 2. These framework stages are all built
on user empathy and are not necessarily a
sequential process; instead, they can be ap-
proached in iterative cycles, adapting to the
demands of a given project.
Figure 2: The ﬁve stages, design thinking frame-
work.
The global design company, IDEO, has de-
veloped the three core activities framework
[24]. The ’three core activities’ is a broad,
ﬂexible framework capturing the design pro-
cess with just three main descriptive phases:
inspiration, ideation, and implementation.
Figure 3 shows and describes the frame-
work.
Figure 3: Three core activities, design thinking
framework.
IBM’s Enterprise Design Thinking is guided
by The loop [22]; a continuous cycle of three
primary phases: observe, reﬂect, and make.
The framework is displayed in Figure 4.
This approach intends to reduce concerns
about making an incorrect decision, encour-
age trying out ideas, and embrace an exper-
imentation approach by undertaking mul-
tiple loops - even after making a decision,
thereby emphasizing rapid iteration.
Figure 4: The loop, design thinking framework.
Google Ventures has created the GV de-
sign sprint, a sprint framework that breaks
the design process down into ﬁve condensed
phases: understand, diverge, decide, proto-
type, and validate [54]. Each phase is ded-
icated to a day, as illustrated in Figure 5,
making it a fast-paced process lasting ﬁve
days corresponding to a working week. This
eﬃcient approach aims for fast decision-
making, quickly moving from a problem to
a tested solution and bypassing the need for
a lengthy and costly process.
Figure 5:
GV design sprint, design thinking
framework.
Lastly is the design framework used in de-
sign and innovation engineering, the double
diamond [11].
This framework consists of
two diamonds, each consisting of a diver-
gent and convergent phase, making it four
phases, namely, discover, deﬁne, develop,
and deliver. The ﬁrst diamond explores the
problem space, whereas the second diamond
explores the solution space. The framework
di↵ers from the other frameworks with its
clear distinction between problem and solu-
tion spaces. Figure 6 visually represents the
double diamond.
p. 7 of 80
==PAGE:10==
2.1
Design Processes
2
BACKGROUND
Figure 6:
Double diamond, design thinking
framework.
Comparing these ﬁve design thinking frame-
works, it becomes evident that there are
some functional similarities. This is despite
the fact that the frameworks’ descriptions
intend to capture their uniqueness.
Focusing on what sets the frameworks
apart, the ﬁve stages and GV design sprints
frameworks o↵er a more detailed approach
compared to other frameworks. This is es-
pecially in contrast to the three core ac-
tivities and the loop, which are extremely
broad and ﬂexible frameworks. The GV de-
sign sprint especially stands out from the
others due to its strict and compressed ﬁve-
day time-bound day-by-day structure.
Despite the di↵erences among the frame-
works, there are notable parallels in their
stages.
The ﬁve stages, three core activi-
ties, the loop, and GV design sprint steps
are compared with the double diamond’s
phases, analyzing the frameworks in the
light of the double diamond.
This analy-
sis, presented in Figure 7, includes a shared
description underscoring the frameworks’
alignment: Despite the non-sequential na-
ture of some of the frameworks, their phases
largely translate to those of the double di-
amond.
An exception is the three core
activity, where a phase is dedicated to
project launch, which typically extends be-
yond the double diamond’s scope.
More-
over, the comparison showcases how each
framework incorporates elements of diver-
gence and convergence, even though this is
only visually represented in the double dia-
mond framework.
Figure 7: Interpretation of design thinking frameworks in light of the double diamond framework.
p. 8 of 80
==PAGE:11==
2.1
Design Processes
2
BACKGROUND
In addition to the framework analysis, Rikke
Friis Dam and Teo Yu Siang [50] describe
ten design thinking frameworks and high-
light six common traits among them:
1.
Empathy towards all stakeholders to ensure
the solutions align with needs. 2. Refram-
ing challenges to gain fresh perspectives and
foster holistic solutions. 3. Divergent think-
ing in the early stages encouraging fast and
many solutions. 4. Convergent thinking to
reﬁne solutions into viable options. 5. Cre-
ating and testing prototypes to identify and
resolve issues. 6. Iterating, revisiting em-
pathy, and potentially redeﬁning challenges
based on new insights.
This study combines the ﬁndings from the
comparative analysis of frameworks shown
in Figure 7, Norman’s revisited perspective
on design thinking in his text ’Rethinking
Design Thinking’ [37] and the examination
of Dam and Siang’s common traits among
design thinking frameworks [50]. This com-
parison led to an extraction of four central
principles of design thinking, despite its var-
ious deﬁnitions and interpretations.
These principles are identiﬁed as the core
to enhance innovation potential. The prin-
ciples are 1. A user-centered approach, 2.
Finding the root problem, 3. Iterating, and
4. Keeping a holistic view. These key prin-
ciples of design thinking are displayed in
Figure 8 followed by an elaboration of the
principles.
Figure 8: Identiﬁed and extracted key principles
of design thinking.
A user-centered approach: Positioning
the user at the center of the design process.
This entails that essential decisions revolve
around real user needs and enhance user
experiences.
In practice, this is realized
through user insights and feedback.
Finding the right problem or need: Ex-
ploring the problem or need before jumping
to exploring and deﬁning the solution. This
involves daring to critically assess whether
the given problem is the root problem or a
symptom of a deeper concern. This point
is also emphasized in Norman’s article ’Re-
thinking Design Thinking’ [37].
Iterating:
Daring to make rapid deci-
sions and thus rapid failures with a begin-
ner’s trial-and-error mindset. This mindset
embraces fast adaptability when the out-
come does not align with one’s expectations
rather than ﬁnding out after investing in an
untested idea.
Holistic thinking:
Acknowledging that
the world is complex and interconnected.
This makes the future an unpredictable
ﬁeld, so one must acknowledge the unknown
factors and consider the broader context.
Conclusively, given that across design think-
ing frameworks, there are some undeniable
similarities, as argued with the framework
comparison in Figure 7 together with the
key principles of design in Figure 8, it sug-
gests that opportunities identiﬁed within
one framework could transition to another.
This means that a solution for applying gen
AI to one framework would likely apply to
others as well.
p. 9 of 80

2.1
Design Processes
2
BACKGROUND
2.1.2
Steps of the Design Process
This subsection strives to outline the de-
signers’ as-is journey, capturing the steps
of the design process that a solution must
align with. The double diamond framework
is used for this exploration for clarity and
coherence.
The exploration of the typical
steps in the double diamond framework is
leveraged by revisiting courses throughout
the design and innovation education.
The Design and Innovation Engineering
DTU program, spanning both BSc and
MSc levels (from Fall 2018 to Spring 2023),
has resulted in the completion of 14 DTU
courses that employed the double diamond
framework. These courses include: 41010,
User-oriented design; 41020, Product analy-
sis and redesign; 41612, Product Design and
Documentation; 42042, User involvement
in process development; 41084, Biologically
inspired design; 41031, Industrial design 1;
S42; Analysis of Brøg co↵ee machine, B42,
A waste sorting system for small Danish
homes - with focus on sustainability; 42087,
Design Methods and Reﬂective Practice;
42502, Facilitating innovation in multidis-
ciplinary teams; 42090, Holistic design of
engineering systems; 41073, Development
and operation of product-service systems;
41628, Conceptualization, and 42081, Stag-
ing co-creation and creativity
An analysis of frequently used and e↵ective
methods was conducted by reviewing the
14 courses listed.
These methods are or-
ganized by their typical application phase
in Figure 9, illustrating the as-is journey.
Note that the method can be used in di↵er-
ent double diamond phases, and the ﬁgure
only outlines their typical appliance based
on experience.
Additionally, the methods
are grouped under umbrella terms that rep-
resent their primary functions, such as con-
ducting ﬁeldwork.
These terms cover the
method’s overall goals; that said, a sin-
gle method can serve multiple functions.
For instance, interviews contribute to both
conducting ﬁeldwork and building empa-
thy, yet are only listed under conducting
ﬁeldwork.
This categorization serves as a
general framework rather than a rigid step-
by-step guide. It is worth mentioning that
the steps of the as-is journey are based on
personal experiences and may carry a per-
sonal bias towards methods proven e↵ective
in practice, primarily within product devel-
opment.
In conclusion, Figure 9 provides a zoomed-
out overview of the as-is design process,
showcasing the existing workﬂow of a de-
signer without the integration of gen AI.
The overview is internally zoomed-out, sum-
marizing various methods rather than dis-
playing one detailed design process, which is
better suited for the identiﬁcation of speciﬁc
process pain points. This zoomed-out ver-
sion acknowledges the diversity in method
selection and combination across di↵erent
design projects, as the methods used in the
design process often vary from project to
project.
That said a design process typically goes
through Figure 7’s identiﬁed steps: Diverg-
ing by uncovering assumptions, conducting
ﬁeldwork, building empathy, and identify-
ing challenges and opportunities. Converg-
ing through synthesizing and scoping. Di-
verging (again) through ideation and explo-
ration of challenges and opportunities. To
converge by synthesizing, reﬁning, detailing,
evaluating and sharing. In reality, the steps
are not as strictly deﬁned or placed, and the
process is always more iterative in real life.

2.2
Generative
Artiﬁcial
Intelli-
gence
This section establishes a foundational un-
derstanding of AI’s capabilities, behaviors,
and evolution. This knowledge is essential
for e↵ectively assisting designers in lever-
aging the beneﬁts of gen AI while being
aware of its limitations. To achieve this, the
section delves into the fundamental compo-
nents of AI, its operational mechanisms,
training processes, and historical progres-
sion.
To explore the concept of artiﬁcial intelli-
gence, it’s necessary to ﬁrst clarify the term
’intelligence’. Human intelligence, with its
complexity, encompasses a range of cogni-
tive processes including emotional, verbal,
logical, artistic, and social intelligence. Psy-
chologist Raymond B. Cattell notably cate-
gorizes human intelligence into two distinct
types: crystallized and ﬂuid intelligence, a
division that provides a foundational un-
derstanding for delving into artiﬁcial intel-
ligence [4]. This classiﬁcation is further il-
lustrated in Figure 10.
p. 11 of 80
==PAGE:14==
2.2
Generative Artiﬁcial Intelligence
2
BACKGROUND
Figure 10: Cattell’s division of intelligence in
crystallized and ﬂuid.
People tend to believe machines master
crystallized, logical, and fact-based intel-
ligence, whereas humans outperform ma-
chines in ﬂuid, creative, and abstract, intel-
ligence. AI challenges this intuitive division
in human and machine capabilities by per-
forming both crystallized and ﬂuid tasks.
Alan Turing published a famous paper in
1950 titled ’Computing Machinery and In-
telligence’ [53], in which he posed the ques-
tion of whether machines can think.
”We may now consider the ground
to have been cleared, and we are
ready to proceed to the debate on
our question, ’Can machines
think?” — Alan Turing, British
mathematician, October 01, 1950
[53]
Turing’s paper became famous for ques-
tioning whether a computer deserves to be
called intelligent if it can trick a human into
believing it is human, suggesting an imita-
tion game today known as the Turing test.
The Turing test is a theoretical framework
to determine machines’ capability to create
text that is indistinguishable from a human-
written text. If a machine can convincingly
imitate human responses, it demonstrates
intelligent behavior, according to Turing.
The Turing Test, evaluating whether a ma-
chine is intelligent, leads to the deﬁnition of
machine intelligence or artiﬁcial intelligence
(AI). AI refers to machines engineered to
”think” like humans: AI covers machines
mimicking human mind actions. This could
be the ability to understand complex con-
tent, learn from experience, engage in a con-
text, problem-solve, and make rational deci-
sions. Accordingly, AI refers to a machine’s
ability to simulate human intelligence. AI
covers various methods and approaches that
aim to develop intelligent machines, mean-
ing machines’ capacity to perform tasks that
otherwise require human intelligence.
Gen AI is a subset of AI that goes beyond
understanding, repeating, predicting, and
analyzing existing data.
Instead, gen AI
uses algorithms to develop new content,
such as audio, code, images, text, simula-
tions, and/or videos. This means that gen
AI refers to a machine’s ability to create
and innovate.
Gen AI is AI technology that can create
new content. This could be text, code,
image, sound, or video.
2.2.1
AI Foundational Overview
This section provides a brief explanation of
AI technologies. It aims to clarify the fun-
damental components and mechanisms that
enable AI systems to perform tasks that
typically require human intelligence.
AI’s necessary building blocks are at the
core of understanding gen AI, starting with
data science.
Data science involves col-
lecting, cleaning, analyzing, and transform-
ing data to extract knowledge and insights.
Embedded within data science is machine
learning,
which utilizes algorithms that
learn from data to make predictions or deci-
sions without being explicitly programmed
for each task.
An essential subset of ma-
chine learning is deep learning.
Deep learning operates on artiﬁcial neu-
ral networks, which, as the name implies,
are layered algorithms inspired by the hu-
man brain’s neural architecture. These net-
works can handle and ﬁnd patterns in large
and complex datasets, enabling the per-
formance of human-like activities, such as
speech recognition or object identiﬁcation
in images. Deep learning is a foundational
pillar in the evolution of AI and gen AI, es-
sential for complex tasks like object recogni-
p. 12 of 80
==PAGE:15==
2.2
Generative Artiﬁcial Intelligence
2
BACKGROUND
tion and generation. It masters the ability
to identify relevant data, e.g., objects in
an image, despite variations in position-
ing, lighting, and context, all while accu-
rately maintaining details such as propor-
tions and unique characteristics [33]. The
foundational principles of neural networks
are explained and visualized in Appx. 01.1,
whereas the Transformer model architec-
ture, which is fundamental in advancing
gen AI, is explained and visualized in Appx.
01.2. Figure 11 provides a visual overview
of the relation of the mentioned AI building
blocks.
Figure 11: AI’s building blocks.
Essential for neural networks is their ability
to ’learn’ through training. A training pro-
cess typically involves presenting the net-
work with extensive datasets, enabling it to
identify patterns, make decisions, and pre-
dict outcomes. This learning process can be
supervised or unsupervised. In supervised
learning, the network is fed data alongside
the correct outputs, allowing the system
to predict and validate its accuracy.
The
system uses the error di↵erences between
the network’s predictions and the correct
outputs to adjust the network’s internal
parameters (the weights and biases of the
network, Appx. 01.1) based on the error of
its predictions; this process is called back-
propagation [33]. This training method sys-
tematically enhances the network’s preci-
sion through iterations, improving its pre-
dictive performance accuracy over time.
The quality and representativeness of the
data are critical, as poor quality or non-
representative data can severely degrade
the system’s performance. This can result
in unreliable or inaccurate output.
This
emphasizes the need for training on large
datasets to ensure better accuracy and fair-
ness of the system’s outputs. However, it
is resourceful and, thus, expensive to train
large data sets with supervised data.
Unlike supervised learning, unsupervised
learning identiﬁes patterns in data without
pre-existing labels and any human interven-
tion.
Instead of having a correct output,
the network is trained based on the data’s
patterns, structures, or distributions [23].
Ultimately, the performance of neural net-
work systems depends on the data they are
trained on, underscoring the critical inﬂu-
ence of training data traits since these will
automatically be traits of the models built
on the neural network: Training data traits,
including biases, are directly transferred to
the models being trained, impacting their
behavior. This link between data and neu-
ral network systems is highlighted in a 2017
thesis from the MIT library [7]. The the-
sis examines the face recognition systems of
IBM, Microsoft, and Face++ and reveals
that they all perform better on light skin
tones than on dark skin tones. Moreover,
the systems perform better on males than
on females. The study found that the pro-
grams were least accurate on dark-skinned
females.
For the best-performing system,
darker females were 32 times more likely
to be misclassiﬁed. This bias is not unique
to facial recognition systems, and as pre-
trained models become more prevalent, it is
crucial to be aware of such challenges.
Accordingly, biases and topic focus within
the datasets inﬂuence the model’s responses
and knowledge scope. AI tools trained on
di↵erent datasets will understand model
inputs, called prompts, di↵erently.
For
p. 13 of 80
==PAGE:16==
2.2
Generative Artiﬁcial Intelligence
2
BACKGROUND
instance, tools trained on scholarly arti-
cles versus tools trained on social media
texts will respond di↵erently to the same
prompt, reﬂecting the tone and context of
their training material. For instance, Chat-
GPT 3.5 and 4.0 respond di↵erently to a
prompt [40].
This variation necessitates
adapting prompts based on the AI model.
Conclusively, the e↵ectiveness of prompts is
fundamentally tied to the speciﬁc algorithm,
changing with each training iteration of a
model.
Moreover, the development of AI
systems goes toward a more intuitive under-
standing of natural language, whereas the
newest GPT4 [40] already shows promising
results in understanding prompts.
There-
fore, creating a guide for prompt engineer-
ing would be an unsustainable solution,
given its limited applicability across vari-
ous AI models and the fact that the opti-
mal prompting method will likely shift with
ongoing model updates.
2.2.2
Evolution of AI
The aim of this subsection is to understand
AI’s progression by outlining a historical
overview of AI’s key milestones and innova-
tions that have inﬂuenced its development.
From the chronological timeline of histori-
cal AI milestones, it becomes evident that
AI technology is not new but has experi-
enced exponential progress in capabilities
that have pushed it into the spotlight. The
timeline below showcases the gen AI tools
by highlighting their launch dates in pink.

1943 Groundwork for
Neural Networks:
Neurophysiologist
Warren McCulloch and Self-taught ed-
ucator and librarian Walter Pitts pub-

nent in Nervous Activity’, which concep-
tualized the brain and laid the ground-
work for neural networks (neural net-
works are explained in Appx. 01.1). [30]
October 01, 1950 Turing test: The
British mathematician Alan Turing pro-
posed the famous ’imitation game’, the
Turing test, to evaluate a machine’s abil-
ity to master intelligent behaviour. The
test has faced criticism for its limitations
in truly assessing machine intelligence.

programmed self-learning game of check-
ers on TV. [2]

advanced image and video-generating tools.
The milestone overview highlights the shift
from AI technology milestones to the de-
velopment of gen AI models. This shift is
shown by the color change from blue to pink.
A notable change came with the launch
of ChatGPT in 2022.
ChatGPT quickly
became the most widely known gen AI tool
and the fastest-growing application of all
time, highlighting gen AI’s ability, with over
1 billion users by March 2023 [31]. Chat-
GPT stood out for its impressive capabili-
ties in understanding natural language and
generating responses that feel remarkably
human. Also, due to its built-in safety mea-
sures that are designed to make interactions
more helpful, truthful, and less prone to bi-
ases (Challenges explained in Section 2.2.1).
This section showcases a rapid progression
within AI technology, demonstrating a need
for the study solution to be adaptable to en-
sure long-term relevance within the rapidly
changing AI landscape. This AI landscape
is expected to continue evolving with the in-
troduction of many new gen AI tools. The
following section, Section 2.3, delves into
some design-relevant gen AI tools.
2.3
Generative AI Tools
It is important to understand the capabili-
ties of gen AI tools to position gen AI op-
portunities within a design process. To do
so, this section explores gen AI tools rele-
vant to design and classiﬁes them according
to their main purposes.
It is nearly impossible to analyze all states
of gen AI tools, with the enormous and
continuously growing amount of existing
gen AI tools. Gen AI tools include numer-
ous agents, AI plug-ins, and AI copilots.
Anyone can create a customized Genera-
tive Pre-trained Transformer (GPT) model
without any required coding skills. OpenAI
even provides a guide on making customized
ChatGPT-based GPTs on their website, ei-
ther public or private, ﬁne-tuned for speciﬁc
purposes [45].
To explore the broad arena of gen AI in de-
sign, research is done through simple Google
searches on ’gen AI tools in design,’ followed
by reviews of the tool-speciﬁc websites and
rapid testing of the tools available.
This
exploration revealed the wide usage of gen
AI tools for diverse purposes.
For clarity, the tools are categorized by
their primary purposes, where some tools
have overlapping purposes as visualized in
Figure 12. This ﬁgure has drawn inspira-
tion from a development arena model [18],
visualizing overlapping purposes through
overlapping colored circles, methods specif-
ically referred to as actor worlds. Each ﬁg-
ure tool is brieﬂy described, with detailed
input, output, pros, and cons in the market
research provided in Appx. 02.1.
p. 15 of 80
==PAGE:18==
2.3
Generative AI Tools
2
BACKGROUND
Figure 12: Overview of categorized gen AI tools sample as of 13 February 2024.
It is essential to point out that this is a
snapshot of the tools reﬂecting their cur-
rent state at the moment of creation of this
ﬁgure (February 13, 2024). This picture will
likely change with the rapid advancements
in AI, as concluded in Section 2.2.2. For ex-
ample, OpenAI’s ChatGPT [44] is evolving
to incorporate DALL-E [42] for image gen-
eration and will likely evolve to incorporate
video animations with their newly intro-
duced gen AI Sora [41] that was launched
two days after this ﬁgure was created. In
other words, the tools may expand or evolve
in capabilities and, thus, purpose while new
tools are expected to come.
In light of the constant development of
tools, making lasting tool-based recommen-
dations for the design process is highly chal-
lenging, as these quickly become outdated if
not constantly tested and updated. There-
fore, this thesis prioritizes making sugges-
tions based on the tools’ purposes to pro-
tect the solution’s relevance over time. By
focusing on tool purposes such as text gener-
ation, image creation, or video production,
the recommendations are more protected to
remain relevant despite how individual AI
tools evolve.

LITERATURE REVIEW
3
Literature Review
This literature review aims to explore cur-
rent research relevant to the intersection of
AI and design, aiming to get a foundational
’state of the art’ knowledge of studies al-
ready conducted and to avoid redundant
work and thus ensure the originality of this
study. This is done by conducting searches
on ’AI in Design,’ ’AI evolution,’ and ’AI-
driven design’ through ’DTU Findit’ [27]
and ’Google Scholar.’ The searching quickly
revealed a necessity of searching by publica-
tion date, as older studies were found out-
dated given the rapid evolution and adop-
tion of AI, which made it necessary to focus
on recent studies. Study recommendations
from personal and academic connections
supplement the literature searches. Among
these is an AI expert within the Implement
consulting group, Adam Hede. The study
recommendations have been numerous due
to the topic’s widespread popularity and
many connections within the design ﬁeld.
This approach is taken to ensure a broad
and updated collection of sources. However,
it was challenging to determine the comple-
tion of the search due to the extensive ma-
terial available in the ﬁeld. Consequently,
it was necessary to set a limited time frame
for this process, constrained by the over-
all project timeline. Ideally, additional time
and energy could have been allocated to this
phase, enhancing the depth of the literature
exploration.
Table 2 provides a curated, concise overview
of relevant studies. The table presents a se-
lection of studies, with their themes and key
ﬁndings, ranked according to their publica-
tion time. As a result of the search by date,
the table exclusively represents relatively re-
cent studies. The key ﬁnding of each state
of study has been highlighted in blue in the
table.

computing power, with Gemini Ultra and possibly
GPT-5 as examples. Cheaper AI models:
Continuously price drops for running gen AI
models are expected due to declining token costs
and increased competition, making eﬃcient AI
solutions a↵ordable and accessible. AI models
are and will continue to interact: With many
inﬂuential and interconnected changes, it is hard
to predict the overall outcomes for businesses.

phasize an ethical, user-centered approach

Conclusively, state-of-the-art research out-
lines AI’s impact on design, revealing its ca-
pacity to enhance quality and productivity
in design-related tasks. This study aims to
equip designers with these AI advantages to
enhance their design work. However, the lit-
erature recognizes AI challenges, including
privacy concerns, bias, and transparency,
advocating for responsible AI-human sym-
bioses gained with a human-centered ap-
proach. This necessary focus on responsi-
ble AI use will be prioritized in this study’s
solution development.
While the state of
studies o↵ers a robust foundation for AI’s
potential in design and emphasizes the im-
portance of a human-AI partnership, they
lack detailed strategies for implementing AI
tools for designers. Addressing this gap is
the primary aim of this thesis and, thus, its
contribution to the ﬁeld.
4
Final Problem Statement
This section’s aim is to use insights from the
state-of-the-art review to evaluate the ini-
tial research question: How might we help
designers integrate and beneﬁt from gen AI
in the design process?
The Literature Review, in Section 3, reveals
an absence of concrete strategies for inte-
grating AI into the design process in the
current literature.
This gap serves as an
area of focus for this thesis in identifying
opportunity spaces for gen AI integration
within a design process.
p. 22 of 80
==PAGE:25==
4
FINAL PROBLEM STATEMENT
Furthermore, insights from Section 2.1.1
suggest that a solution applicable within
one design framework is likely adaptable to
other frameworks.
This ﬁnding results in
choosing the double diamond framework as
a familiar representative of a design process
and, thus, the focus of this thesis.
Mak-
ing this choice acknowledges that the dou-
ble diamond framework predominantly fo-
cuses on the initial design stages rather than
the maintenance and management phases.
Therefore, choosing the double diamond
framework may emphasize the early design
phases.
The initial research underscores AI’s ex-
tensive and complex nature in design, high-
lighting the necessity to reﬁne the focus.
This leads to the formulation of a Final
Problem Statement for this Master’s study:
Final
problem
statement
(FPS):
How might we create a minimum viable
prototype (MVP) of a solution that helps
designers identify gen AI opportunities
within the double diamond framework?
This problem statement is broken down into
several focused sub-questions to ensure the
FPS is answered. The reasoning for choos-
ing these sub-questions is explained in the
continuation of the question.
1. What are typical steps and design
methods of a double diamond process?
Identifying common phases and meth-
ods within the double diamond pro-
vides a baseline for opportunity spaces
for gen AI integration.
2. What are designers’ perspectives on
AI, including hopes and concerns? As-
sessing designers’ attitudes towards
AI reveals opportunity or resistance,
which is crucial for designing the
MVP.
3. What potential impact do text gener-
ators have on productivity and quality
when applied to sub-tasks?
Understanding the potential inﬂuence
of gen AI on sub-tasks helps specify
areas where gen AI will enhance or
harm the design work to make con-
crete use suggestions. The speciﬁc fo-
cus on text generators on sub-tasks
provides insight into the capabilities of
gen AI tools while ensuring the scope
remains manageable.
4. Where within the double diamond do
opportunities for integrating gen AI
lie?
Understanding where gen AI
can o↵er value is necessary for rec-
ommending opportunities within the
double diamond framework for design-
ers. And is the identiﬁed gap in the
state of the literature.
5. How can gen AI opportunities e↵ec-
tively be communicated to designers -
what are e↵ective media to do so? Ef-
fective communication of gen AI op-
portunities is essential to ensure the
solution’s value.
6. What is an MVP for a solution help-
ing designers identify gen AI oppor-
tunities within the double diamond?
Conceptualization of a tangible MVP
that addresses identiﬁed opportunities
and concerns illustrates the solution’s
practical application.
The sub-questions cover essential aspects,
including typical design process phases, de-
signers’ perceptions of AI, the impact of
text generators on sub-tasks, opportuni-
ties for gen AI integration within the dou-
ble diamond framework, e↵ective mediums
for communication to designers, and con-
ceptualizing an MVP. These sub-questions
provide a solid foundation for creating a so-
lution.
Systematically
addressing
these
sub-
questions clariﬁes the path towards creating
an MVP while ensuring that the solution is
grounded in actual user needs and work-
ﬂows of designers, enhancing the potential
for successful integration and adoption.

5
METHODOLOGY
5
Methodology
This section provides a detailed descrip-
tion of the study’s procedure to answer the
FPS and corresponding sub-questions de-
scribed in Section 4.
This detailed look
at the study’s framework reﬂects on which
methods will contribute to answering the
FPS and, thus, which methods are applied
for project progression. This is substituted
with where, within the master’s paper, these
sub-questions are answered.
The study not only targets the double dia-
mond framework [11] through its FPS but
also adopts the double diamond framework,
navigating through diverging and converg-
ing phases. In this process, a design brief
marks the turnover point from the problem
space to the solution space.
The double
diamond approach emphasizes exploration
and deﬁnition before moving into solution
development, allowing for continuous reﬁne-
ment based on new insights and learnings.
The process is highly iterative and allows
for responsive adaptation to new insights.
It applies a bottom-up approach, ensuring
the MVP is built on solid foundations be-
fore making decisions.
Adapting a design thinking framework em-
phasizes a user-centered approach, cf. Sec-
tion 2.1.1, to ensure innovation potential.
Throughout the study, there is a focus on
the users’ needs, which is ensured by build-
ing the foundation on real user needs and
early-stage validating the concepts with po-
tential users.
Figure 13 illustrates the methodology of
this master’s thesis, detailing What speciﬁc
FPS sub-questions are addressed during
which double diamond phase, How these
questions are tackled using various methods,
and Where within the thesis each method
is documented. The methods corresponding
to the sub-questions are indicated with the
sub-questions number, highlighted in blue,
providing a comprehensive overview of the
research approach. The documentation of
the methods within the thesis is written in
orange, and the project’s steps are grey.
Overall, the project’s problem space (ﬁrst
diamond) navigates through collecting em-
pirical data, identifying challenges and op-
portunities, synthesizing insights, and deﬁn-
ing the scope. The solution space (second
diamond) goes through ideation, identiﬁca-
tion of challenges and opportunities, synthe-
sis of solutions, and evaluation of the ﬁnal
MVP suggestion. The methods are chosen
for their e↵ect contributing to these steps.
The steps are based on the paper’s analysis
of typical double diamond steps, extracted
in Section 2.1.2. As the initial phases are
more comprehensive than initially expected,
the process is heavy towards the ﬁrst dia-
mond, leaving the solution space less re-
ﬁned.
Initially, the project is diverging with a
mixed-methods
data
approach,
merging
qualitative and quantitative data collection
through desk research, questionnaires, and
interviews, allowing for a nuanced under-
standing of designers’ content, needs, in-
cluding their experiences, and the practical
implications of gen AI tools in their design
processes. To do so, experts within design,
namely designers, are asked about their de-
sign processes, AI- perspective, -experience,
-advantages, and -concerns.
This qualita-
tive data, enriched by quantitative mea-
sures, lays the groundwork for identifying
patterns and speciﬁc needs within the de-
sign ﬁeld, guiding the direction of the solu-
tion.
The project takes an experimental approach
to answer sub-question 2, exploring the im-
pact of text generators. This involves a pilot
trying to replicate an MIT study on Chat-
GPT’s impact on writing tasks combined
with applying ChatGPT to this thesis’ de-
sign steps to evaluate the e↵ect.
p. 24 of 80
==PAGE:27==
5
METHODOLOGY
Figure 13: Methodological Overview: Linking double diamond phases, FPS sub-Questions, research
methods and paper sections.
The ﬁndings of the problem space form a
design brief [47] setting the direction for
the solution. From this brief, a brainstorm-
ing session is conducted to generate ideas,
laying the groundwork for conceptualiza-
tion.
To ensure a diverse range of ideas,
this session is carried out as a co-creative
workshop with family members, focusing on
generating numerous ideas and prioritizing
quantity over quality.
This approach also
beneﬁts quality time with family in a period
where time is scarce.
The concepts are represented as testable
MVPs to evaluate communication media
with designers. Engaging designers in pro-
totype testing o↵ers initial validation and
guides the development of a more reﬁned
prototype addressing the FPS. The de-
tailed MVP is evaluated using the universal
virtues from the product idea model [19],
examining the MVPs potential based on
the problem’s scope, solution maturity, user
needs, technological feasibility, and business
viability.
In summary, this thesis’ undergoes a user-
centered iterative process from need deﬁni-
tion to MVP testing and evaluation, con-
tinuously evaluating and adapting.
This
process involves various methods combining
empirical research, experimental applica-
tion, and iterative development.
This ap-
proach establishes a solid foundation for a
solution, introducing designers to actionable
insights about gen AI’s potential in design
processes.

6
IMPACT OF TEXT GENERATORS
6
Impact of Text Generators
This section evaluates text generators’ im-
pact on various processes, addressing sub-
question three of the FPS. It employs an
experimental approach, applying ChatGPT
4.0 in selected processes. ChatGPT 4.0 was
chosen as a representative of text genera-
tors due to its foundational role in devel-
oping many other text generators. Utilizing
its latest version aligns with the study’s aim
to be forward-looking in a rapidly evolving
ﬁeld, ensuring relevance and insight into the
future potential of text generation technolo-
gies.
6.1
ChatGPT in Writing Tasks
As a part of exploring the potential of Chat-
GPT’s impact, This study joined Imple-
ment Consulting Group in conducting a
pilot study investigating ChatGPT’s inﬂu-
ence on journalism.
Despite the apparent
di↵erences, journalism and design converge
on fundamental aspects closely related to
the core principles of design thinking iden-
tiﬁed in Section 2.1. These aspects include
a user-centered focus, an iterative process,
and the task of identifying core issues within
complex systems while maintaining a holis-
tic perspective.
Both ﬁelds prioritize ef-
fective communication; journalists aim to
present information clearly and engagingly,
whereas designers focus on creating experi-
ences that resonate with users’ needs and
aspirations.
A crucial commonality is the
reliance on interviews; journalists use them
to capture diverse viewpoints and informa-
tion, while designers conduct interviews to
uncover user needs, challenges, and prefer-
ences.
These parallels suggest how Chat-
GPT’s impact on journalism o↵ers valuable
insights for understanding its potential in-
ﬂuence on design processes.
This pilot study took place within a large
media and entertainment company.
It
speciﬁcally explored the impact of intro-
ducing ChatGPT on journalists’ workﬂow,
focusing primarily on productivity and qual-
ity. The pilot was conducted from the 7th
to the 25th of August 2023, in collaboration
with Implement consultants.
While pilot
results and data were collectively discussed
and reviewed, the data analysis and conclu-
sions were primarily drawn independently
by Implement and the author of this paper
due to di↵ering objectives for the pilot.
The pilot’s motivation is inspired by a study
conducted by Noy and Zhang from the Mas-
sachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT),
published in March 2023, titled ’Experi-
mental Evidence on the Productivity E↵ects
of Generative Artiﬁcial Intelligence,’ [38].
demonstrated signiﬁcant gains in productiv-
ity and writing quality through the use of
ChatGPT, summed up in Findings 1.
Findings 1 MIT Study [38]
• An increase in productivity was
observed, as indicated by a re-
duced time spent on tasks by 0.8
standard deviations (SDs).
• The output quality showed im-
provement, increasing by 0.4 SDs.
Quality was measured by blinded
professionals using criteria like
writing, content quality, and orig-
inality.
• ChatGPT
was
especially
bene-
ﬁcial for participants who ini-
tially demonstrated lower levels of
productivity, thereby making the
workplace more equitable in terms
of productivity.
• Participants
reported
increased
job satisfaction, indicating a more
enjoyable and fulﬁlling work expe-
rience.
• The experiment created a shift in
working tasks—participants spent
less time drafting and more time
editing.
p. 26 of 80
==PAGE:29==
6.1
ChatGPT in Writing Tasks
6
IMPACT OF TEXT GENERATORS
The MIT research evaluated the e↵ect of
ChatGPT on 444 college-educated profes-
sionals across diverse ﬁelds such as mar-
keting, data analysis, and management and
showed remarkable results. The MIT study
o↵ered several compelling insights, which
the pilot aims to replicate key elements of.
This pilot can thus be considered a down-
scaled replica to investigate whether similar
gains in productivity and quality are achiev-
able in the realm of journalism.
6.1.1
Pilot Approach
The pilot media and entertainment com-
pany operates across multiple Danish cities
and involves 19 journalists from around
Denmark who contributed to the pilot data.
Among these, ﬁve journalists were observed
by a team of external observers, including
the author of this study.
The remaining
14 journalists self-measured their time. All
19 journalists contributed to the business
intelligence (BI) data used in the analysis.
The pilot analyzes three data sources: 1.
self-reported data, 2.
observations of ﬁve
journalists by ﬁve individuals (two consul-
tants, two Media and Entertainment com-
pany development editors, and the study
author), and 3. BI data.
The pilot spanned over three weeks, illus-
trated in Figure 14, with elaborating expla-
nations of the weeks.
Figure 14: The pilot’s three-week approach.
Week 1 | Baseline Study: The ﬁrst week
serves as a baseline, collecting data through
questionnaires, observations, and BI to un-
derstand journalists’ typical workﬂows. In
addition to the baseline data, Year-To-Date
(YTD) data is used, which encompasses in-
formation from the beginning of the year
through the end of week 1.
Together the
YTD data includes a total of 33 weeks with
a total of 25 journalists. This foundational
data will later provide a reference point for
comparisons to Week 2, where ChatGPT is
introduced.
Week 2 | ChatGPT Study:
The sec-
ond week starts with a three-hour ChatGPT
training session led by Adam Hede, an AI
expert from Implement consulting Group.
Following the training, journalists get access
to ChatGPT 4.0, the latest version avail-
able as of August 2023. Data are once again
collected using questionnaires, observations,
and BI to assess any shifts in workﬂow or
productivity. This approach allowed for the
testing of ChatGPT’s potential with writing
experts, namely journalists who interacted
with ChatGPT throughout the process.
Week 3 | Self-Driving Study:
In the
third week, journalists are encouraged to
work independently with ChatGPT 4.0
without observation. However, only ﬁve of
ﬁfteen journalists responded to this week’s
questionnaires, and none utilized ChatGPT,
invalidating the data.
The third week highlighted a need for longer
training and testing periods to e↵ectively
adopt tools, reﬂected by disappointing and
missing responses.
This conﬁrms Imple-
ment’s chairman Stig Albertsen in his well-
known quote:
”Culture eats strategy for
breakfast” -Stig Albertsen,
Chairman of Implement
In summary, this pilot approach employs a
three-week structure to evaluate the integra-
tion of ChatGPT 4.0 into journalistic work-
ﬂows, capturing data from questionnaires,
observations, and BI metrics.

6.1.2
Pilot Analysis and Results
Let’s shift focus to analyzing the potential
impact of ChatGPT 4.0 in journalism, as
earlier argued, can reﬂect its impact on de-
sign processes.
Initially, the journalist’s workﬂow is divided
into the time allocated for content creation
and other work-related activities.
Con-
tent creation includes various tasks, namely
’Theme Selection,’ ’Fieldwork,’ ’Writing,’
’Revising,’
and ’Follow-up.’
Tasks not
falling within this category are labeled as
’Other Activities.’ The categorization is vi-
sually represented in Figure 15, elaborating
on the tasks based on the questionnaire re-
sponses (Appx. 03.1 and 03.2) and conver-
sations during observations. It is also worth
mentioning that the content creation pro-
cess is non-linear, like the design process, as
journalists often juggle multiple stories con-
currently, shifting frequently among various
tasks.
Figure 15: Interpretation of journalists’ work-
ﬂow, indicated as the content creation process.
Figure 16 analyzes the time distribution
among these di↵erent tasks, illustrating the
hourly comparison with and without Chat-
GPT. The data comes from the baseline
study of week 1 compared to the ChatGPT
study in week 2 and is provided in Appx.
03.3.
The ﬁgure (Figure 16) reveals a signiﬁcant
di↵erence between observed hours and those
reported in the questionnaires.
This dis-
crepancy likely arises from multiple factors:
the questionnaires account for a full work-
week, whereas observations are limited to
Monday to Thursday; additionally, some
journalists may spend time on tasks out-
side the observed oﬃce hours or may have
misconceptions about the time used. To ad-
dress these factors, an analysis of the per-
centage distribution of tasks is conducted,
as depicted in Figure 17.
Figure 17 shows the percentage distribution
comparison. The questionnaire answers re-
veal negligible changes in task distribution
between weeks 1 and 2, a variance lower
than what might naturally occur week-over-
week. In contrast, observational data show
a marked reduction in time allocated
to content creation tasks, dropping from
74% to 57%. This divergence between ques-
tionnaire responses and observational data
may stem from the subjective and imprecise
human perception of time. Therefore, while
questionnaire responses are taken seriously
in this study, they are not necessarily taken
literally, as the observational data provide a
factual account of what actually transpired.
”What people say, what people
do, and what people say they
do are entirely di↵erent things.” -
Margaret Mead, anthropologist
Therefore, this study distinguishes between
’say-evidence,’ based on opinions and be-
liefs, and ’do-evidence,’ rooted in observable
events. Given this, greater weight is given
to the ’do-evidence’ represented by obser-
vations and YTD data.
The Do-evidence
indicates a reduction in time allocated to
content creation.
To understand how the
distribution of tasks within the content cre-
ation process is a↵ected, Figure 18 examines
the content creation process alone.

Alongside these promising outcomes, three
success stories stand out:
1. A journalist presented two headline
options to an editor: one by the jour-
nalist and the other by ChatGPT. Un-
aware that ChatGPT had generated
one of the headlines, the editor se-
lected the ChatGPT-produced head-
line over the one crafted by the jour-
nalist. This short story draws refer-
ences to and passes the Turing test
by tricking a human into believing its
content is human-made (The Turing
test is described in Section 2.2).
2. An experiment with a ChatGPT-
created social media post led to a no-
table increase in engagement, likes,
and comments compared to earlier
posts.
3. An article primarily written by Chat-
GPT
quickly
became
one
of
the
second week’s top-performing pieces,
highlighting the tool’s e↵ectiveness
in generating compelling written con-
tent.
These stories highlight ChatGPT’s poten-
tial to create headlines, enhance social me-
dia posts, and create engaging text content.
The identiﬁed potential within the con-
tent creation process is depicted in Figure
23, supported by direct quotations from
journalists, within the ﬁgure.
These po-
tential areas are based on analyzing the
stages in the content creation process. The
tools were useful for the journalists, for
instance, where journalists consistently em-
ployed ChatGPT, without it being for ex-
perimental purposes, but as an integral part
of their content creation workﬂow. This is
in combination with where journalists re-
ported the tool’s usefulness in their self-
reported questionnaires, as documented in
Appendices 03.1 and 03.02.
However, the journalists also express con-
cerns. Among these is a fear that it will re-
sult in lay-o↵s due to AI-driven productivity
gains. Another concern is a fear of losing hu-
man touch and authenticity in AI-generated
content, with its overly positive and less nu-
anced language, losing the critical edge es-
sential to journalism. The journalists fur-
ther express concerns about the accuracy
and credibility of AI-generated content and
emphasize the importance of maintaining
quality and thorough fact-checking.
Ad-
ditionally, the potential divide between AI
users and non-users in a workplace points
to worries about workplace equality; e↵orts
must be made to ensure inclusive access to
training and resources. These concerns col-
lectively underscore a need for careful con-
sideration of both the practical and ethical
implications of integrating gen AI into work
processes.
Figure 23: Identiﬁed ChatGPT potential within
the content creation process.
The pilot demonstrates signiﬁcant Chat-

some overlapping with the design ﬁeld, par-

This, on the other hand, counts for ’writ-
ing’, which is essential to both ﬁelds.
In
design, writing includes student reporting
and professionals creating client materials,
insight catalogs, pitches, social media posts,
funding searches, and presentations.
Overall, the pilot delivers impressive re-
sults.
When compared to insights from
the MIT study, it successfully replicates
increased productivity, output quality, job
satisfaction, and a shift in time allocation,
with less time spent on the writing process
and more on reviewing and editing. These
results are summarized in Findings 2.
Findings 2 Pilot Study
• An increase in productivity, with less time spent on content creation, while more
content is created without compromising with quality, contributing to an increase
of 33-36%; see Figure 22.
• A shift in task allocation shows reduced time spent on writing and increased time
on revising. See ﬁgure 18.
• An increase in content output: A 16-19% increase in articles per journalist per
week and a 17-32% surge in word count per journalist per week. See Figure 19.
• An increase in quality: A 4% increase in full reads, a 2-20% increase in the pro-
duction of highest-priority articles, and substantial 71-103% growth in subscrip-
tions/conversions. See Figure 20.
• A behavioral shift among journalists emerges as they begin to perceive AI less
as an automation tool, skilled in crystallized intelligence, and more as a creative
collaborator. Source Appx 03.01, 03.02, and from the conversations at site.
• An increase in participant satisfaction, from 7.4/10 to 7.8/10, representing a 4.3%
improvement. source self-reported questionnaires see Appx 03.3.

lot is based on some assumptions. Table 3
outlines these assumptions, listed according
to their impact, along with an evaluation of
their signiﬁcance if they prove inaccurate.

6.2
ChatGPT Application in Design Tasks
6
IMPACT OF TEXT GENERATORS
The table reveals considerable ChatGPT
advantages for eﬃciency, accessibility, and
process support.
However, its limitations
of accuracy, depth, and originality necessi-
tate careful integration into design methods.
Some key points worth highlighting from the
experimentation is its ability to understand
fast-written, rambling text despite spelling
mistakes and poor sentence structure.
The adoption of ChatGPT in design work-
ﬂows notably shifts the focus towards the
more engaging aspects of design by quickly
generating initial drafts and synthesizing
data. It helps to overcome writing blocks
and thus increase productivity. This indi-
cates a transformation in the design tasks,
aligning with Adam Hede’s insight on the
impact of ChatGPT on reducing time spent
on less stimulating tasks.
”One day we will look at the tasks
that we do today and see them as
inhuman tasks (refers to tedious
tasks)”
- Adam Hede, AI expert within
Implement consulting group,
August 15, 2023
ChatGPT showed particular value when
working alone, serving as a sparring partner
for contextual guidance and intermittent
feedback throughout a project. Its role as
a collaborator, allowing for inputs, critical
reviewing, and support, has signiﬁcantly en-
hanced the eﬃciency, quality, and joy of this
study’s individual work. However, its time
generating answers interrupts the working
ﬂow, making it easy to lose focus.
Another notable strength is its capacity to
create awareness of its limitations, for in-
stance, by highlighting areas that require
further investigation,
acknowledging un-
certainties, or declining to provide poten-
tially harmful information. This reﬂects a
trend towards greater transparency about
tool weaknesses, as it is more noticeable in
ChatGPT 4.0 than in its previous version,
ChatGPT 3.5, suggesting future iterations
will continue to enhance user awareness.
Additionally, it allows reﬁning or chang-
ing prompts if a request is misunderstood.
If ChatGPT gets o↵-topic, starting the
prompt all over again may be more e↵ective
than trying to get back on track. Moreover,
it was found that the quality of responses
increases through extended conversations,
demonstrating an improved ability to pro-
vide relevant and speciﬁc information with
an understanding of project nuances.
It appeared that many of the use cases mir-
rored those identiﬁed in the pilot study,
detailed in Section 6.1. These included as-
sisting with creating initial drafts, reﬁning
language, composing social media content,
and enhancing and reviewing report text.
There are many perspectives on ChatGPT.
During the pilot study, Adam Hede, an AI
expert within Implement, described Chat-
GPT as an intern eager to help with an-
swers, an intern who does exactly what one
asks, neither more nor less, who persistently
follows instructions even if it means fabri-
cating them.
Meanwhile, Sam Rahbar, a
fellow student who recently undertook a
special course on testing AI tools in fall
2023, characterized ChatGPT as a hyper-
autistic team member with vast objective
knowledge but lacks the human touch. This
study proposes viewing ChatGPT as a ded-
icated structured team member who is al-
ways awake and eager to contribute yet
unable to initiate tasks. This member ex-
cels in a collaborative environment, with
the weakness that the team member never
truly vouches for the reliability of own sug-
gestions.
The learnings from applying ChatGPT are
analyzed in light of ChatGPT’s inﬂuence on
design methods. These insights are commu-
nicated in Findings 3.
p. 37 of 80
==PAGE:40==
7
DESIGNERS’ NEEDS
Findings 3 ChatGPT’s impact on design methods, extracted from experiments
• Enhances eﬃciency by making ﬁrst drafts: Speeds up the process of creating ini-
tial drafts on tasks such as market research, desk research, interview questions,
questionnaires, and reporting. This reduces the entry barrier, thereby accelerating
a quicker start.
Along with eﬃciently categorizing, generating overviews, sum-
maries, and categorizations.
• Reduction in tedious tasks: Reduces time spent on monotonous tasks, such as
organization of data, categorizing information, creating tables, and summarizing
ﬁndings. Allowing designers to focus on creativity and innovation.
• Enhance idea generation: Contributes with many ideas and creative suggestions.
This diversity in thought and perspective can lead to innovative solutions and ap-
proaches within the design process.
• Necessitates Critical Reviewing: Ethical dilemmas, such as biases and copyright
issues, demand careful consideration and thoughtful navigation, and potentially
new norms for design work.
Secondly, over reliance on ChatGPT for creative input, can lead to lack of origi-
nality or depth. Necessitating designers to critically assess and reﬁne content to
ensure originality and relevance.
• Improve quality: Eﬃcient in making critical reviews, o↵ering language reﬁnement
and structure suggestions, the capability to o↵er immediate feedback allows for
a more dynamic and iterative design process. Designers can reﬁne concepts and
prototypes more quickly, leading to a more agile and responsive design methodology.
In summary, the ﬁndings from the exper-
iments with ChatGPT in design methods
reveal text generators’ potential to revolu-
tionize the design process, making it faster,
more eﬃcient, and potentially more inno-
vative. However, designers must apply text
generators cautiously to ensure that the use
does not compromise ethical considerations,
originality, or depth.
Before outlining the speciﬁc area for gen AI
integration in the double diamond frame-
work, one must understand what the de-
signers’ needs are and the potential impli-
cations of introducing gen AI within their
design process.
7
Designers’ Needs
The purpose of this section is to analyze de-
signers’ underlying needs and ensure that
the proposed solutions align e↵ectively with
these requirements.
The identiﬁed pat-
terns will be encapsulated in the creation
of personas, providing a clear representa-
tion of the uncovered user needs and prefer-
ences. The approach is to systematically or-
ganize and evaluate the unstructured ﬁeld-
work data to identify patterns in the de-
signers’ current workﬂows and stated gen AI
perceptions and use cases. This analysis is
based on insights from 14 interviews and 23
questionnaire responses (See Appx. 05).
p. 38 of 80

Diverse Designer Respondents
7
DESIGNERS’ NEEDS
7.1
Diverse Designer
Respondents
A designer covers various roles across vari-
ous ﬁelds. The goal is to develop a solution
that is inclusive between design ﬁelds. To
achieve this goal, the interviews and ques-
tionnaires represent designers from di↵er-
ent ﬁelds, professions, and companies. The
following section will elaborate on which
designers are represented in interviews and
questionnaires.
The gender distribution is shown in Figure
24. There is a higher male representation
in the interviews, whereas more females re-
sponded to the questionnaires. This results
in an almost equal, balanced representation
of both genders.
Figure 24: Gender distribution among designers
in the sample.
The age distribution is investigated in Fig-
ure 25.
The most represented group is
young adults under 30 years old, constitut-
ing 29 out of 37 responses, making up 79%
of the total responses.
This bias towards
younger age groups should be considered
when designing, aiming for inclusive solu-
tions across all age groups.
Figure 25: Age distribution among designers in
the sample.
The high representation of young adults
makes it interesting to explore the propor-
tion of students or professionals in the de-
sign industry.
Figure 26: Professions distribution among de-
signers in the sample.
Figure 26 reveals that out of 37 respon-
dents, 21 (or 57%) are students. A signiﬁ-
cant majority of these student respondents,
76% (16 students), are pursuing master’s
degrees, with the same proportion (76%)
being from the Technical University of Den-
mark (DTU), many of whom are enrolled
in Design and Innovation programs.
The
result is a bias toward design students, par-
ticularly those from DTU. The remaining
ﬁve student respondents are from Aalborg
University (AAU), Lund University, Danish
School of Media and Journalism, IT Uni-
versity (ITU), and Copenhagen Business
Academy (CBH).
Eight of these student respondents are ac-
tively involved in the design industry, of-
fering valuable insights from both academic
and professional standpoints.
The total
number of respondents in the design indus-
try is eight students, two educators, and
11 industry professionals. They are adding
up to 21 out of 37 (57%), equivalent to the
same amount involved at universities, with
more students working half-time with a stu-
dent job. The presented companies include
a mix of startup companies, design agencies,
consultancies, and well-established Danish

DESIGNERS’ NEEDS
companies, such as Rockwool, Soundboks,
Four Design, Oticon, Novo Nordisk, and
LEGO.
The types of designers and their areas
of expertise, as self-identiﬁed by the re-
spondents, are outlined in descending or-
der of frequency.
These include product-,
product/service-, system-, UX-, service-,
UI-, industrial-, graphic-, communication-,
multimedia-, furniture-, and organizational
designers. Together, the designers encom-

A
signiﬁcant representation of product and
product/service designers is expected, con-
sidering the substantial number of design
engineers and startup companies in the sam-
ple (see respondents’ answers in Appx. 05.1
and 05.3).
Respondents are questioned about their
design processes to understand the design
landscape in which the solution must ﬁt.
As found in Section 2.1 about the design
process, these vary not only among dif-
ferent designers but also according to the
speciﬁc project. However, certain common
core principles consistently appear regard-
ing what designers consider important in a
design process.
One repeated element is a user-oriented
design thinking mindset, which often in-
cludes conducting user interviews and in-
volving users, customers, and/or clients in
the design process.
Furthermore, numer-
ous designers embrace the importance of
thorough groundwork to establish a foun-
dational understanding of the problem or
need before diving into solutions.
Another recurrent theme is the iterative ap-
proach. Many designers highlight the value
of multiple iterations in their work, viewing
this approach as essential for continuous re-
ﬁnement and improvement.
These shared
practices reinforce and align with the key
principles of design thinking, as found in
Section 2.1.1.
The diverse design process further implies
a diverse use of design programs. Let’s ex-
amine the range of design programs utilized
by the designers. Figure 27 displays the top
ten used design programs.
Figure 27:
Design program uses distribution
among designers in the sample.
Adobe Illustrator is the most popular tool,
used by 73% of the respondents. This pref-
erence highlights Illustrator’s importance in
the design process, likely due to its impres-
sive property for vector graphics, which are
essential in various design ﬁelds. Figma fol-
lows closely, with 65%, reﬂecting its popu-
larity in collaborative UI/UX design due to
its user-friendly interface, prototyping fea-
ture, and real-time collaboration features.
Subsequently, Miro, yet a real-time collabo-
rative tool, was utilized by 54% of the re-
spondents.
Figma and Miro’s popularity
reﬂects a broader trend toward tools that
enhance teamwork and creative planning,
demonstrating the collaborative dynamics
in design processes. PowerPoint’s usage by
half of the respondents highlights its con-
tinued relevance for presentations and po-
tentially also for its ease of use in creating
quick layouts and mockups.
Adobe Pho-
toshop and InDesign also show signiﬁcant
usage, which indicates the continued rele-
vance of graphic design tools for image cre-
ation and layout design.
The pattern of
design program usage reﬂects designers’ di-
verse needs and workﬂows and indicates a
trend toward tools that o↵er versatility, col-
laboration, and eﬃciency in the design pro-
cess.
